                    }
                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome of sweet orange ( Citrus sinensis )"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2012 45:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2012-11-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2012 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Yijun Ruan and colleagues report the draft genome of the sweet orange, Citrus sinensis. Their data suggests sweet orange originated from a cross between pummelo and mandarin. Oranges are an important nutritional source for human health and have immense economic value. Here we present a comprehensive analysis of the draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). The assembled sequence covers 87.3% of the estimated orange genome, which is relatively compact, as 20% is composed of repetitive elements. We predicted 29,445 protein-coding genes, half of which are in the heterozygous state. With additional sequencing of two more citrus species and comparative analyses of seven citrus genomes, we present evidence to suggest that sweet orange originated from a backcross hybrid between pummelo and mandarin. Focused analysis on genes involved in vitamin C metabolism showed that GalUR, encoding the rate-limiting enzyme of the galacturonate pathway, is significantly upregulated in orange fruit, and the recent expansion of this gene family may provide a genomic basis. This draft genome represents a valuable resource for understanding and improving many important citrus traits in the future."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Oranges are an important nutritional source for human health and have immense economic value. Here we present a comprehensive analysis of the draft genome of sweet orange (<i>Citrus sinensis</i>). The assembled sequence covers 87.3% of the estimated orange genome, which is relatively compact, as 20% is composed of repetitive elements. We predicted 29,445 protein-coding genes, half of which are in the heterozygous state. With additional sequencing of two more citrus species and comparative analyses of seven citrus genomes, we present evidence to suggest that sweet orange originated from a backcross hybrid between pummelo and mandarin. Focused analysis on genes involved in vitamin C metabolism showed that <i>GalUR</i>, encoding the rate-limiting enzyme of the galacturonate pathway, is significantly upregulated in orange fruit, and the recent expansion of this gene family may provide a genomic basis. This draft genome represents a valuable resource for understanding and improving many important citrus traits in the future.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Citrus</i> is a large genus that includes several major cultivated species, including <i>C. sinensis</i> (sweet orange), <i>Citrus reticulata</i> (tangerine and mandarin), <i>Citrus limon</i> (lemon), <i>Citrus grandis</i> (pummelo) and <i>Citrus paradisi</i> (grapefruit). In 2009, the global citrus acreage was 9 million hectares and citrus production was 122.3 million tons (FAO statistics, see URLs), which is the top ranked among all the fruit crops. Among the 10.9 million tons (valued at $9.3 billion) of citrus products traded in 2009, sweet orange accounted for approximately 60% of citrus production for both fresh fruit and processed juice consumption (FAO statistics, see URLs). Moreover, citrus fruits and juice are the prime human source of vitamin C, an important component of human nutrition.</p><p>Citrus fruits also have some unique botanical features, such as nucellar embryony (nucellus cells can develop into apomictic embryos that are genetically identical to mother plant). Consequently, somatic embryos grow much more vigorously than the zygotic embryos in seeds such that seedlings are essentially clones of the maternal parent. Such citrus-unique characteristics have hindered the study of citrus genetics and breeding improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Roose, M.L. &amp; Close, T.J. Genomics of citrus, a major fruit crop of tropical and subtropical regions. in Genomics of Tropical Crop Plants (eds. Moore, P.H. &amp; Ming, R.) 187â201 (Springer Press, 2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7761e820">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gmitter, F.G. et al. Citrus genomics. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 611â626 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7761e823">2</a></sup>. Complete genome sequences would provide valuable genetic resources for improving citrus crops.</p><p><i>Citrus</i> is believed to be native to southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Webber, H.J. History and development of the Citrus industry. in The Citrus Industry (eds. Reuther, W. et al.) Chap. 1, 1â39 (University of California Press, 1967)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7761e832">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Scora, R.W. On the history and origin of Citrus. Bull. Torrey Bot. Club 102, 369â375 (1975)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7761e835">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gmitter, F. &amp; Hu, X. The possible role of Yunnan province, China, in the origin of contemporary Citrus species (Rutaceae). Econ. Bot. 44, 267â277 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7761e838">5</a></sup>, and cultivation of fruit crops occurred at least 4,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Webber, H.J. History and development of the Citrus industry. in The Citrus Industry (eds. Reuther, W. et al.) Chap. 1, 1â39 (University of California Press, 1967)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7761e842">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Legge, J. The shoo king and The tribute of Yu. in The Chinese Classics, Vol. 3, Pt. 1 and Pt. 3, Bk. 1, Chap. 6, 111â112 (Trubner &amp; Co., London, 1865)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d7761e845">6</a></sup>. The genetic origin of the sweet orange is not clear, although there are some speculations that sweet orange might be derived from interspecific hybridization of some primitive citrus species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Moore, G.A. Oranges and lemons: clues to the taxonomy of Citrus from molecular markers. Trends Genet. 17, 536â540 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d7761e849">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nicolosi, E. et al. Citrus phylogeny and genetic origin of important species as investigated by molecular markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 1155â1166 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7761e852">8</a></sup>. Citrus is also in the order Sapindales, a sister order to the Brassicales in the Malvidae, making it valuable for comparative genomics studies with the model plant <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</p><p>We aimed to sequence the genome of Valencia sweet orange (<i>C. sinensis</i> cv. Valencia), one of the most important sweet orange varieties cultivated worldwide and grown primarily for orange juice production. Normal sweet oranges are diploids, with nine pairs of chromosomes and an estimated genome size of <span class="stix">â¼</span>367 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d7761e865">9</a></sup>. To reduce the complexity of the sequenced genome, we obtained a double-haploid (dihaploid) line derived from the anther culture of Valencia sweet orange<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cao, H. et al. Doubled haploid callus lines of Valencia sweet orange recovered from anther culture. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 104, 415â423 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d7761e869">10</a></sup>. We first generated whole-genome shotgun paired-endâtag sequence reads from the dihaploid genomic DNA and built a <i>de novo</i> assembly as the citrus reference genome; we then produced shotgun sequencing reads from the parental diploid DNA and mapped the sequences to the haploid reference genome to obtain the complete genome information for Valencia sweet orange. In addition, we conducted comprehensive transcriptome sequencing analyses for four representative tissues using shotgun RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) to capture all transcribed sequences and paired-endâtag RNA sequencing (RNA-PET) to demarcate the 5â² and 3â² ends of all transcripts. On the basis of the DNA and RNA sequencing data, we characterized the orange genome for its gene content, heterozygosity and evolutionary features. The genome and transcriptome analyses presented in this study yield new insights into the origin of sweet orange and the genomic basis of vitamin C metabolism and provide a rich resource of genetic information for citrus breeding and genetic improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>From the dihaploid DNA of sweet orange (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), we generated 785 million high quality paired-endâtag sequencing reads (2 Ã 100 bp) from various DNA fragment sizes (<span class="stix">â¼</span>300 bp, 2 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb) using the Illumina GAII sequencer, representing 214-fold base-pair coverage and 4,889-fold physical coverage of the estimated citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The sequence reads were assembled by SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d7761e894">11</a></sup> and Opera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Gao, S., Sung, W.K. &amp; Nagarajan, N. Opera: reconstructing optimal genomic scaffolds with high-throughput paired-end sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 18, 1681â1691 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d7761e898">12</a></sup>, resulting in 16,890 assembled sequence contigs (N50, 49.89 kb) and 4,811 scaffolds (N50, 1.69 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The total contig sequence length (320.5 Mb) covers 87.3% of the estimated sweet orange genome, and more than 80% of the genome assembly is in 135 scaffolds that are larger than 713 kb and up to 8.2 Mb. To evaluate the accuracy of the citrus genome assembly, we assessed the quality of the scaffolds by BAC clone analysis, alignment to the established citrus genetic linkage map and cytogenetic analysis.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the sweet orange draft genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2472/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A BAC clone library of sweet orange has previously been constructed and characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Baig, M.N., Yu, A., Guo, W. &amp; Deng, X. Construction and characterization of two Citrus BAC libraries and identification of clones containing the phytoene synthase gene. Genome 52, 484â489 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1172">13</a></sup>, and the estimated size range of BAC clone inserts was 120 kb. We picked individual clones for BAC-end capillary sequencing. Of the 735 pairs of BAC-end sequence reads in which both ends of a pair were mapped within the same contig, the average distance was 125,875 Â± 26,788 bp (s.d.). This is consistent with the previously estimated length of BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Baig, M.N., Yu, A., Guo, W. &amp; Deng, X. Construction and characterization of two Citrus BAC libraries and identification of clones containing the phytoene synthase gene. Genome 52, 484â489 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1176">13</a></sup>, suggesting the correctness of the contig assembly. To further evaluate the accuracy of the assembly on a local scale, we analyzed seven complete BAC sequences of sweet orange that are available in GenBank and also anchored in the sweet orange genetic map. The alignments between these BAC sequences and the citrus genome assembly showed an overall identity of 97.6% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Together, the BAC clone sequencing analyses validated that the assembly is of high quality.</p><p>We then asked whether the assembly of the genome sequences matches well with the established sweet orange genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lyon, M.P. A Genomic Genetic Map of the Common Sweet Orange and Poncirus trifoliata. PhD dissertation, University of California, Riverside, (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1189">14</a></sup>. We mapped the scaffolds to 768 markers with known sequences in the genetic linkage groups, and 160 scaffolds (each &gt;10 kb) were anchored, accounting for 239 Mb of the assembled citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Furthermore, 83 scaffolds that accounted for the majority of the length of the anchored scaffolds (178.7 Mb, or 75% of total anchored scaffolds) were in matched orientation within the genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>), suggesting high alignment accordance between the anchored genetic markers and the sequence scaffolds. The discordance in alignment could reflect technical accuracy, such as insufficient marker density, genotyping errors or individual variations between the sequenced genome (Valencia sweet orange) and the genetic map that was based on three crosses between three sweet orange cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lyon, M.P. A Genomic Genetic Map of the Common Sweet Orange and Poncirus trifoliata. PhD dissertation, University of California, Riverside, (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1199">14</a></sup>. Valencia orange is known to have chromosome rearrangements relative to seedy sweet oranges<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Iwamasa, M. Reciprocal translocation in the Valencia orange. Chromosome Inform. Service 4, 9â10 (1963)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1203">15</a></sup>. Combining the assembled genome sequences and the genetic linkage groups, we constructed the pseudomolecules for each of the nine chromosomes and ordered them on the basis of genetic length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Alignment of the genome sequence assembly with the genetic map of C. sinensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Alignment of the genome sequence assembly with the genetic map of <i>C. sinensis.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="354"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Assembled scaffolds (blue; 239 Mb, or 75% of the assembled genome sequence) were anchored in the nine linkage groups (LG1âLG9, yellow) with the corresponding genetic markers (black bars). The pseudochromosome numbers are assigned on the basis of the estimated length of the genetic linkage groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lyon, M.P. A Genomic Genetic Map of the Common Sweet Orange and Poncirus trifoliata. PhD dissertation, University of California, Riverside, (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1226">14</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We then took some cytological markers, including two ribosomal genes (45S and 5S) and a tandem repeat sequence (107 bp), for FISH analyses to further validate the accordance of the assembled citrus genome sequences. The cytologically marked chromosomes showed tentative consistence with the genomically defined pseudochromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 4</a>). For example, pseudochromosomes 1 and 8, bearing the 45S region at proximal locations, correspond to two type-B chromosomes, with one in the fragile site (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>); pseudochromosome 6, with the mapped scaffolds containing 45S and 5S ribosomal DNA sequences at distal regions, corresponds to the type-D chromosome with a fragile site; and chromosome 7 contains a large number of copies of the 107-bp tandem repeat sequence at two distal locations, which corresponds to the type-C chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Collectively, our cytological experiments provided sequence-matched evidence for chromosomes 1, 6, 7 and 8. Additional cytological evidence is needed for other chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome characterization</h3><p>Most plant genomes are featured with highly repetitive regions with transposable elements and unique gene-coding regions. We analyzed the citrus genome in these two aspects to reveal the basic structural and functional features of the genome.</p><p>Transposable elements are major components of plant genomes and important contributors to genome evolution. However, very few transposable elements have previously been identified in <i>Citrus</i>. To analyze the transposable elements of the orange genome, we first constructed a custom digital transposable element library using <i>ab initio</i>, homology- and structure-based transposable element prediction tools and then applied this dataset to annotate the citrus reference genome. Such analysis led to the identification of 61.7 Mb of sequence in the assembled contigs, accounting for 20.5% of the <i>C. sinensis</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 5</a>). This level of repetitiveness is similar to those of <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1275">16</a></sup> and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Goff, S.A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp japonica). Science 296, 92â100 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1280">17</a></sup>, suggesting that sweet orange has a relatively compact genome.</p><p>Class I long terminal repeat retrotransposons occupy over 89% of the transposable elements, consisting of 198 Copia families and 130 Gypsy families. Similar to other compact genomes such as those of <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Du, J. et al. Evolutionary conservation, diversity and specificity of LTR-retrotransposons in flowering plants: insights from genome-wide analysis and multi-specific comparison. Plant J. 63, 584â598 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1289">18</a></sup> and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gao, L., McCarthy, E.M., Ganko, E.W. &amp; McDonald, J.F. Evolutionary history of Oryza sativa LTR retrotransposons: a preliminary survey of the rice genome sequences. BMC Genomics 5, 18 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1293">19</a></sup>, the intact elements in each family have less than 250 members, whereas more than 90% of long terminal repeat retrotransposon families contain only one to five full-length elements. Estimation of insertion time indicated that more than 80% of the intact elements were amplified within the last 2 million years, and 10% are younger than 50,000 years (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 6</a>). In contrast, class II DNA transposons only comprised <span class="stix">â¼</span>11% of the transposable elements, similar to in the apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1300">20</a></sup> and papaya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1304">21</a></sup> genomes. Among the class II DNA transposons, miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) occupied 67% of the sequence. Notably, we identified a new type of MITE, named MiM (MITE inserted in microsatellite), in the citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p><p>To annotate the citrus genome for protein-coding genes, we used a comprehensive strategy that combined <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, protein-based homology searches and experimental supports (ESTs, RNA-Seq and RNA-PET) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). We used four <i>ab initio</i> gene-finding programs (Fgenesh, GeneID, Genscan and GlimmerHMM) on the repeat-masked genome sequences. In addition, we collected 917,610 proteins from the UniProt database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Dimmer, E.C. et al. The UniProt-GO Annotation database in 2011. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D565âD570 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1324">22</a></sup> and 958,121 <i>Citrus</i> EST reads from GenBank and generated 964.6 million RNA-Seq reads from four citrus tissues (callus, leaf, flower and fruit) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a>). In total, we identified 29,445 protein-coding loci (gene models) with 44,387 transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>), and 23,421 gene models were allocated in nine pseudochromosomes. The gene transcripts have an average length of 1,817 bp, a mean coding sequence size of 1,255 bp and an average of 5.8 exons per gene. The RNA-Seq and EST data suggested that 26% of the gene models (7,640 gene loci) encoded two or more transcript isoforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 5a</a>). Overall, 85% of the gene models contain multiple exons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 5b</a>), and 99% of the predicted coding sequences and 80% of the splice junction sites were supported by the RNA-Seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 7</a>), showing the high accuracy of gene annotation in the orange genome. We evaluated and corrected all predicted gene models using the RNA-PET sequencing data (which is complementary to the RNA-Seq data) for demarcating the gene boundaries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). We generated approximately 100 million RNA-PETs from four tissues (callus, leaf, flower and fruit) and used them for mapping, clustering and annotation of the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a>), confirming 70% of the 5â² and 3â² boundaries of the gene transcripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Notably, we identified 967 transcripts for 744 putative gene models solely on the basis of the combined RNA-Seq and RNA-PET data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>), representing a collection of potentially new and citrus-specific genes. In addition, we identified a total of 181 ribosomal RNAs, 451 transfer RNA, 39 small nucleolar RNAs and 104 small nuclear RNAs in the citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). We also identified 40 conserved microRNA (miRNA) families with 227 members in the orange genome on the basis of previous small RNA sequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu, Q. et al. Discovery and comparative profiling of microRNAs in a sweet orange red-flesh mutant and its wild type. BMC Genomics 11, 246 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1376">23</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 10</a>), and most of the miRNA genes are distributed evenly across the genome, with several small clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome characterization."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome characterization.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1014"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Circular diagram depicting the genomic landscape of the nine sweet orange pseudochromosomes (Chr1 to <span class="stix">â¼</span>Chr9 on a Mb scale). The denotation of each track is listed on the right. RPKM, reads per kilobase exon model per million mapped reads. (<b>b</b>) Experimental data support for gene model predictions. Left, box plot of RNA-Seq reads aligned over gene features. The top and bottom of the boxes indicate the upper and lower quartiles, respectively. The middle red bars represent the median, and the red dots denote the mean value for each feature. CDS, coding sequence. Right, Venn diagram of the predicted RNA splice junctions supported by various evidences (EST, protein and RNA-Seq data). Numbers in parentheses are the number of splice junctions in each category. (<b>c</b>) Demarcation of gene model boundaries by RNA-PET data. Histogram plot of RNA-PET data in association with aligned gene models in relation to the 5â² (putative transcription start site, TSS) and 3â² (putative poly(A) site, PAS) boundaries.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To better understand citrus-specific biology, we identified citrus-specific genes. We performed comparative analyses of the orange genome against 21 other plant genomes and screened them by EST and peptide sequences in the public database (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). Of the 29,445 protein-coding genes in the orange genome, 23,804 were grouped into 14,348 gene families, with 1,691 genes being specific to citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Most (96%) of the citrus-specific genes are 'hypothetical' and have unknown function. Of the 58 annotated genes, the over-represented protein domains are the zinc finger transcription factor domain and the nucleotide-binding-site and leucine-rich-repeat (NBS-LRR) domain, which contain potential disease-resistance genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Heterozygosity and hybrid origin of sweet orange</h3><p>Heterozygosity is a common feature in most eukaryotic organisms and has important biological functions in crops. Although citrus trees are generally perceived to have highly heterozygous traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gmitter, F.G. et al. Citrus genomics. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 611â626 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1436">2</a></sup>, the amount of heterozygosity in the whole genome is not clear. Sequencing of the haploid and diploid genomes provided the genomic data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 12</a>) for us to comprehensively assess the heterozygosity of sweet orange. From the diploid genome sequencing data, we identified 1.06 million SNPs and 176,953 small insertions/deletions (indels). More than 80% of the SNPs and indels were anchored on the nine pseudochromosomes. The overall polymorphism density is 3.6 SNPs and 0.6 indels per kb in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Approximately one-third of the SNPs were located in genic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 14</a>), and 16,843 gene loci (72%) contained at least one SNP (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Considering a 0.52% error rate in detecting SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>), we set up a stringent criterion of five or more SNPs per gene to reliably call variant alleles; using this criterion, 12,297 gene loci (53%) were considered to harbor variant alleles (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 15</a>). This observation suggests a notably high heterozygosity rate in the gene loci.</p><p>To verify this observation, we genotyped 307 simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers on the dihaploid and parental diploid of sweet orange and identified 155 (50%) SSR loci in a heterozygous state (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 16</a>), which is similar to the heterozygosity rate in the protein-coding gene loci found by SNP analysis. Moreover, chromosome karyotyping analysis showed that 6 of the 18 chromosomes have no clear counterpart in the parental diploid, whereas the chromosomes were easily grouped into nine pairs in the dihaploid line (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>), further supporting the observation that the diploid genome of sweet orange is highly heterozygous.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Heterozygosity and hybrid origin of sweet orange."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Heterozygosity and hybrid origin of sweet orange.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="503"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The heterozygosity rate of the sweet orange genome. Homozygous and heterozygous states of genic regions were detected using SNP data from the parental diploid genome; repeat regions were evaluated by comparison of SSR marker patterns between the diploid and dihaploid. The number in the top pie chart indicates the gene number, and the number in the bottom pie chart is the number of SSR markers. (<b>b</b>) Chromosome karyotyping of dihaploid (left) and parental diploid (right). The 18 chromosomes (chrs) in each nucleus were easily identified into nine matched pairs in the dihaploid line, whereas the six chromosomes in the diploid nucleus have no clear counterpart, indicating high heterozygosity in the diploid state. The cytological types of chromosomes were traditionally named as Bf, B, C, Df, D and F according to the 4â²-6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (blue) and chromomycin A3 (yellow) banding regions, as well as the fragile site (circle), as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Guerra, M. Cytogenetics of Rutaceae. V. High chromosomal variability in Citrus species revealed by CMA/DAPI staining. Heredity 71, 234â241 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1489">41</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). (<b>c</b>) SSR marker genotyping of two pummelo cultivars (P1 and P2), sweet orange (SO) and two mandarin cultivars (M1 and M2). Of the 307 SSR markers, 105 were shared among pummelo, orange and mandarin (middle), 55 were pummelo hereditary (left) and 147 were mandarin hereditary (right). One marker showed an orange-specific pattern and was excluded in this analysis. (<b>d</b>) The genetic origin of the sweet orange dihaploid genome determined by high-density SNP markers of pummelo and mandarin. OP, origin pattern (pummelo origin in yellow, mandarin origin in orange and undetermined in gray); SC, assembled sequence scaffold; LG, linkage group map. Pseudochromosome 1 is shown here as an example, and the other chromosomes are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Figure 11</a>. (<b>e</b>) A model of the sweet orange origin. With pummelo (PP) as the female parent crossed with mandarin (MM), the interspecific hybrid was backcrossed with mandarin (MM) and produced the ancient sweet orange.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We thus investigated the speculated hybrid origin of sweet orange from pummelo (<i>C. grandis</i>) and mandarin (<i>C. reticulata</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Moore, G.A. Oranges and lemons: clues to the taxonomy of Citrus from molecular markers. Trends Genet. 17, 536â540 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1526">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Nicolosi, E. et al. Citrus phylogeny and genetic origin of important species as investigated by molecular markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 1155â1166 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1529">8</a></sup>, which are two primitive species of <i>Citrus</i>. To measure the possible genetic contribution to sweet orange from pummelo and mandarin, we surveyed the genotype of sweet orange, two pummelo cultivars and two mandarin cultivars using sweet orangeâderived SSR markers and identified 202 polymorphic markers, 55 of which are pummelo hereditary and 147 of which are mandarin hereditary (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 16</a>). It is remarkable that the numbers of pummelo-hereditary markers and mandarin-hereditary markers (55 and 147, respectively) fit well with a 1:3 ratio (<i>Ï</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.54 &lt; <i>Ï</i><sup>2</sup><sub>0.95</sub> (2â1) = 3.84), indicating an interesting genetic relationship between sweet orange, pummelo and mandarin.</p><p>To comprehensively verify the genome composition of sweet orange inferred from SSR markers, we extended our analysis using high-density SNP makers. For this purpose, we generated whole-genome shotgun short sequence reads for three pummelo cultivars and three mandarin cultivars, each at more than 30-fold coverage of the citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 12</a>); we then mapped the sequences to the haploid orange reference genome and identified 475,450 SNPs common in three individual pummelos and 308,837 SNPs common in three individual mandarins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 17</a>). Using a 100-kb genomic window, we calculated the SNP density preference for pummelo or mandarin and then determined the hereditary origin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>), which allowed us to assign 39.7 Mb of the sweet orange genome from pummelo and 118.2 Mb from mandarin, which approximately fits a ratio of 1:3 in the genomic contributions from pummelo and mandarin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), consistent with the SSR analysis results.</p><p>On the basis of the high heterozygosity rate (50%), the 1:3 ratio of pummelo and mandarin genetic input and the evidence that the chloroplast of sweet orange is probably derived from pummelo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Moore, G.A. Oranges and lemons: clues to the taxonomy of Citrus from molecular markers. Trends Genet. 17, 536â540 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1574">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>), we hypothesized that sweet orange originated from an interspecific hybridization with pummelo as the female parent and mandarin as the male parent followed by a backcross with a male mandarin (sweet orange = (pummelo Ã mandarin) Ã mandarin) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome evolution</h3><p>Characterization and annotation of the citrus genome provided the basic features for us to further investigate the evolutionary history of sweet oranges. Most plant species have gone through whole-genome duplication (WGD) during evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1592">24</a></sup>. The size and frequency of paralogous gene families accumulated in each genome serves as a record for such evolutionary process. Self-alignment of the citrus genome sequences on the basis of the 23,421 gene models located in the nine pseudochromosomes identified 1,296 paralogous gene groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). The mean synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) in the duplicated genes (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> = 1.27) suggested that an ancient WGD occurred in citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Furthermore, we analyzed intergenome collinearity between orange and cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1614">25</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1620">16</a></sup>, apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1624">20</a></sup>, strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1629">26</a></sup> and grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1633">27</a></sup> genomes and identified orange orthologous genes in syntenic blocks with respect to each of these genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 18</a>). A one-to-one orthologous relationship was predominant for the orange-to-cacao, orange-to-grape and orange-to-strawberry comparisons, indicating that there were no recent WGDs except the shared ancient triplication, called the Î³ event, which was shared by all core eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1640">24</a></sup>. In contrast, we found an approximately one-to-two orthologous relationship for the orange-to-apple comparison and a one-to-four relationship for the orange-to-<i>Arabidopsis</i> comparison, supporting that an additional one and two rounds of recent WGD occurred in apple and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Furthermore, on the basis of cross-species synteny block boundaries, we estimated that at least 49 events of chromosome translocations and fusions occurred in the nine citrus chromosomes evolved from chromosomes of a proposed paleohexaploid ancestor that is common to all sequenced eudicot genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 479â487 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1657">28</a></sup>. It is noteworthy that citrus pseudochromosome 4 had only one chromosome translation and fusion event, whereas other pseudochromosomes underwent multiple (3â12) interchromosome fusions during their evolutionary history (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evolutionary analysis of the sweet orange genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Evolutionary analysis of the sweet orange genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="513"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Schematic representation of major interchromosomal relationships within the 1,294 paralogous gene groups in the sweet orange genome. Syntenic blocks derived from the seven ancestral protochromosomes A1, A4, A7, A10, A13, A16 and A19 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 479â487 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1678">28</a>) are color coded as indicated. (<b>b</b>) Distribution of synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) for homologous gene groups for intrachromosome and interchromosome comparisons. Gene duplication analysis in comparison to apple, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and cacao indicates that no recent WGDs occurred in the sweet orange genome. (<b>c</b>) Schematic representation of the syntenic relationship of sweet orange chromosome 9 and the corresponding chromosomes in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Malus</i> Ã <i>domestica</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. Syntenic regions derived from the seven ancestral protochromosomes are color coded as in <b>a</b>. Among the 59 syntenic blocks between sweet orange chromosome 9 and the <i>Arabidopsis</i> chromosomes, 22 (37%) show primary correspondence to four <i>Arabidopsis</i> blocks, 10 to three blocks, 24 to two blocks and 3 to one block; among the 79 syntenic blocks between sweet orange chromosome 9 and the apple chromosomes, 48 (61%) show primary correspondence to two <i>Arabidopsis</i> blocks, 20 to three blocks, 6 to four blocks and 2 to one block. Therefore, on the basis of these percentages, one-to-four and one-to-two relationships were determined to be the most probable for the orange-to-<i>Arabidopsis</i> and orange-to-apple comparisons, respectively. (<b>d</b>) Citrus phylogeny on the basis of 103 single-copy genes shared between <i>Arabidopsis</i>, cacao, poplar, grape, apple, strawberry, papaya and castor bean from nuclear genome data. MYA, million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>This whole-genome sequence also provides comprehensive information for phylogenetic analysis. We applied the maximum likelihood method for genome-scale phylogenic analysis using 103 shared single-copy nuclear genes from orange, <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1750">16</a></sup>, cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1754">25</a></sup>, poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1758">29</a></sup>, grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1762">27</a></sup>, apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1766">20</a></sup>, strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1771">26</a></sup>, papaya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1775">21</a></sup> and castor bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Chan, A.P. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 951â956 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1779">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). The phylogenetic tree showed that <i>Citrus</i> is phylogenetically closer to cacao, <i>Arabidopsis</i> and papaya, although it is ancient compared to them in the Malvidae, and is estimated to have diverged from Malvales 85 million years ago. Thus, our analysis provides new information on the phylogeny of citrus and other plant species on the basis of genome-scale data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genes specially involved in citrus fruit biology</h3><p>Fruit biology is an interesting topic for citrus not only because citrus fruits are an important nutrition source for humans but also because they involve unique metabolic pathways. This citrus reference genome provides a comprehensive genomic framework of genes to help address citrus fruit biology. A clear strategy is to identify genes specifically expressed in fruit tissues. We compared the RNA-Seq data derived from callus, leaf, flower and fruit and identified 2,697 (9.2%) genes that were significantly upregulated (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01) in fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Gene Ontology analysis of upregulated genes in citrus fruits revealed that genes for oxidoreductase, oxidation reduction and vitamin binding were over-represented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 20</a>).</p><p>Fruit ripening is an important biological feature and has tremendous economical value. Genes involved in ripening have been well studied in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1817">31</a></sup>. Of the 16 tomato ripening genes, we found that only 3 (<i>ETR3</i> (also known as <i>NR</i>), <i>MADS-RIN</i> and <i>BP0353</i> (also known as <i>PHYF</i>)) are differentially expressed in orange fruits, whereas the majority (13) are not (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 21</a>), possibly reflecting the existence of two distinct ripening mechanisms for the climacteric (tomato) and nonclimacteric (orange) fruits. Notably, MADS-RIN, a transcription factor previously shown to be necessary for the normal development and ripening of both tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Vrebalov, J . et al. A MADS-box gene necessary for fruit ripening at the tomato ripening-inhibitor (rin) locus. Science 296, 343â346 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1840">32</a></sup> (climacteric) and strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Seymour, G.B. et al. A SEPALLATA gene is involved in the development and ripening of strawberry (Fragaria Ã ananassa Duch.) fruit, a non-climacteric tissue. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 1179â1188 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1844">33</a></sup> (nonclimacteric), is highly upregulated in orange fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 21</a>), providing further support for the notion that MADS-RIN may function as a key-ripening regulator with broad impacts in both climacteric and nonclimacteric fruit ripening.</p><p>We were specifically interested in the biosynthesis of vitamin C (<span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-ascorbic acid (AsA), a strong antioxidant agent), as orange fruits are known to be rich in this vitamin. We analyzed the gene transcription of key enzymes involved in four known biosynthesis branch pathways upstream of AsA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Cruz-Rus, E., Botella, M.A., Valpuesta, V. &amp; Gomez-Jimenez, M.C. Analysis of genes involved in l-ascorbic acid biosynthesis during growth and ripening of grape berries. J. Plant Physiol. 167, 739â748 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1857">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bulley, S.M. et al. Gene expression studies in kiwifruit and gene over-expression in Arabidopsis indicates that GDP-l-galactose guanyltransferase is a major control point of vitamin C biosynthesis. J. Exp. Bot. 60, 765â778 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1860">35</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>) and found that many of these genes are upregulated specifically in fruit. Remarkably, all three genes for the enzymes (<i>PG</i>, <i>PME</i> and <i>GalUR</i>) involved in the galacturonate pathway branch were substantially upregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>), of which the gene encoding <span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-galacturonic acid reductase (<i>GalUR</i>) is the rate-limiting step in this pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Agius, F. et al. Engineering increased vitamin C levels in plants by overexpression of a D-galacturonic acid reductase. Nat. Biotechnol. 21, 177â181 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1886">36</a></sup>. Coincidentally, we identified 18 <i>GalUR</i> paralogous genes in the citrus genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 22</a>), which is the largest number among the plant genomes (17 in apple and grapevine, 15 in strawberry, 13 in papaya, 12 in rice, maize and cocoa, 7 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, 6 in <i>Brachypodium</i> and none in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 23</a>). Further evolutionary analysis using the four closely related plant species within the Malvidae clade (orange, cacao, papaya and <i>Arabidopsis</i>) revealed two clusters (one with four genes and the other with seven genes) of recent <i>GalUR</i> gene expansion in the citrus genome, probably by tandem duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). Notably, these two clusters also showed an expansion mode in papaya, another tree crop with high vitamin C content in its fruit. Noticeably, orange <i>GalUR</i> genes within these two clusters showed diverse expression patterns across different developmental stages. One of them (<i>GalUR</i>-12) was significantly upregulated in fruit (<i>P</i> = 5 Ã 10<sup>â7</sup>), whereas the other three members in the same cluster did not show a similar expression pattern (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#Fig5">Fig. 5a,b</a>), indicating diversification of gene transcription regulation in AsA biosynthesis. <i>GalUR</i>-12 shares high sequence identity (<i>E</i> value &lt; 10<sup>â106</sup>) and shows a close phylogenetic relationship (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>) with the strawberry <i>GalUR</i> gene, which has been further tested for specific vitamin C production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Agius, F. et al. Engineering increased vitamin C levels in plants by overexpression of a D-galacturonic acid reductase. Nat. Biotechnol. 21, 177â181 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1949">36</a></sup>. Collectively, our data indicate that the galacturonate pathway is highly upregulated in orange fruit and <i>GalUR</i> may be the most important contributor to AsA accumulation in orange fruit.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genes involved in vitamin C metabolism."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Genes involved in vitamin C metabolism.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2472/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2472_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="676"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Heat map of the normalized RNA-Seq data for genes involved in AsA metabolism. Genes with a minimum expression level (RNA-Seq RPKM &gt; 1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B.A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621â628 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d7761e1970">42</a>)) are shown. (<b>b</b>) Phylogenetic analysis of the <i>GalUR</i> gene family in <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> (top); two recent expansions are shaded with two oval circles (one cluster with <i>GalUR</i>-6, <i>GalUR</i>-11, <i>GalUR</i>-12 and <i>GalUR</i>-13 (orange oval) and the other cluster with <i>GalUR</i>-1, <i>GalUR</i>-2, <i>GalUR</i>-3, <i>GalUR</i>-4, <i>GalUR</i>-8, <i>GalUR</i>-10 and <i>GalUR</i>-14 (gray oval)). Note that <i>GalUR</i>-12 is a recent member of the family and is highly upregulated in orange fruits. The genomic organization of the 18 <i>GalUR</i> genes (black arrows) in the sweet orange genome is shown (bottom). Sc, scaffold.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2472/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>We sequenced the sweet orange genome using a two-step approach: first, we sequenced and assembled a dihaploid genome, and second, we mapped the parental diploid genomic DNA sequence reads to the haploid reference genome to complete the construction of the heterozygous genome map. The availability of the sweet orange genome sequence provides a valuable genomic resource for citrus genetics and breeding improvement. The sequence of sweet orange, as a member of the Rutaceae family and the Sapindales, also provides a valuable reference for plant genome evolutionary analyses.</p><p>Our genome analysis indicated that there was only an ancient (Î³) triplication event and no recent WGD events in the orange genome, which partially explains the small size of the citrus genome. Phylogenetic analysis indicated that citrus is rather ancient, evolutionarily close to cacao and estimated to have diverged 85 million years ago from Malvales. An infrequent reproductive cycle in some taxa because of apomixis, male and/or female sterility, long juvenility and vegetative propagation may contribute to the restriction of genome expansion and evolution.</p><p>Heterozygosity is of great interest in crop plants. Hybridization causing a high degree of heterozygosity could create hybrid vigor known as heterosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Lippman, Z.B. &amp; Zamir, D. Heterosis: revisiting the magic. Trends Genet. 23, 60â66 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2057">37</a></sup>. The remarkably high degree of heterozygosity in the genome of cultivar Valencia sweet orange (<i>C. sinensis</i> cv. Valencia), as evidenced in our genomic and cytological analyses, hinted that sweet orange is an interspecific hybrid between pummelo and mandarin. On the basis of the collective evidence, we reconstructed the scenario regarding the ancient primary events of the origin of sweet orange: female pummelo crossed with male mandarin to create the initial interspecific hybrid that was further crossed again with male mandarin to produce sweet orange. This event might have happened at least 2,300 years ago, or much earlier, as sweet orange was recorded in Chinese literature as long ago as 314 BC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Webber, H.J. History and development of the Citrus industry. in The Citrus Industry (eds. Reuther, W. et al.) Chap. 1, 1â39 (University of California Press, 1967)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2064">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chinese Society of Citriculture. Citrus Industry in China. (China Agriculture Press, Beijing, China, 2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2067">38</a></sup>. Although additional genetic changes might have occurred afterward, it is still remarkable that this ancient hybrid genotype seems to be preserved in today's sweet orange, probably because of its strict nature of asexual reproduction (apomixis through nucellar embryo) and manmade selection and propagation by grafting. This scenario explains why most of today's sweet orange cultivars are genetically one biotype and highly heterozygous, with diversification occurring mostly through somatic mutations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Moore, G.A. Oranges and lemons: clues to the taxonomy of Citrus from molecular markers. Trends Genet. 17, 536â540 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2071">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Barrett, H.C. &amp; Rhodes, A.M. A numberical taxonomic study of affinity relationships in cultivated Citrus and its close relatives. Syst. Bot. 1, 105â136 (1976)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2074">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Mendel, K. Bud mutations in citrus and their potential commercial value. Proc. Int. Soc.
