Citricult. 1, 86â89 (1981)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2077">40</a></sup>.</p><p>Characterization of the citrus genome for protein-coding genes and comparative genomic analysis with other plant genomes, as well as transcriptome analyses, have yielded several insights into citrus-specific genes, particularly those that are involved in fruit biology and metabolic pathways. Our analysis suggests that the rate-limiting enzyme GalUR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Agius, F. et al. Engineering increased vitamin C levels in plants by overexpression of a D-galacturonic acid reductase. Nat. Biotechnol. 21, 177â181 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2084">36</a></sup> in the galacturonate pathway could be an important contributor to the high vitamin C accumulation in citrus fruit, and the recent expansion of the <i>GalUR</i> gene family in the citrus genome may suggest a genomic basis for regulating vitamin C production.</p><p>The availability of the orange genome and deep mining of orange-specific genes with known or unknown biochemical functions will facilitate a better understanding of many economically and agronomically important traits, including fruit color, flavor, sugar, organic acid and disease resistance in years to come. Future comparison of this sweet orange genome with the recently released high-quality clementine mandarin genome assembly draft (see URLs) should provide new insights into citrus biological and evolutionary questions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">URLs.</h3><p>Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO statistics), <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/default.aspx">http://faostat.fao.org/default.aspx</a>; clementine mandarin genome assembly, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/clementine.php">http://www.phytozome.net/clementine.php</a>; sweet orange genome website, <a href="http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange">http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant material and DNA sequencing.</h3><p>The dihaploid line derived from another culture of C. <i>sinensis</i> cv. Valencia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cao, H. et al. Doubled haploid callus lines of Valencia sweet orange recovered from anther culture. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 104, 415â423 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2139">10</a></sup> was used for paired-endâtag DNA sequencing using an Illumina GAII platform. Approximately 785 million 2 Ã 100 bp reads were generated from libraries with different fragment sizes (300 bp, 2 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb). DNA from the heterozygous diploid parent of the dihaploid line (the tree from which the anther was harvested and the dihaploid line was derived), three pummelos (<i>C. grandis</i>) and three mandarins (<i>C. reticulata</i>) was subjected to whole-genome shotgun sequencing of paired-endâtag reads from 300-bp genomic DNA fragments (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly.</h3><p>We first applied the software QUAKE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kelley, D.R., Schatz, M.C. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Quake: quality-aware detection and correction of sequencing errors. Genome Biol. 11, R116 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2160">43</a></sup> to correct the reads from each library. Contig assembly, scaffolding and gap filling of sequencing data was done using the assembler SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2164">11</a></sup>, with the three DNA-PET libraries (2 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb) being used to link contigs into scaffolds. Scaffolds and contigs were refined further with the gap-filling module in SOAPdenovo (GapCloser), which is used for bridging scaffold gaps, to produce an assembly with a scaffold N50 of 427 kb that covered 286 Mb of the citrus genome. To further improve the assembly, we used the optimal scaffolder Opera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Gao, S., Sung, W.K. &amp; Nagarajan, N. Opera: reconstructing optimal genomic scaffolds with high-throughput paired-end sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 18, 1681â1691 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2168">12</a></sup> with DNA-PET reads (10 kb and 20 kb) and BAC ends (<span class="stix">â¼</span>125 kb, 5,136 BAC-end sequences) in order of increasing library size to construct larger scaffolds and fill gaps using GapCloser (final genome size of 320.5 Mb and N50 of 1.69 Mb). DNA-PET reads and BAC ends were mapped respectively to the SOAPdenovo assembly with BOWTIE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Langmead, B., Trapnell, C., Pop, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Ultrafast and memory-efficient alignment of short DNA sequences to the human genome. Genome Biol. 10, R25 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2172">44</a></sup> and BWA-SW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589â595 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2176">45</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Assembled scaffolds anchoring to genetic map.</h3><p>The <i>C. sinensis</i> genetic map by Lyon <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lyon, M.P. A Genomic Genetic Map of the Common Sweet Orange and Poncirus trifoliata. PhD dissertation, University of California, Riverside, (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2198">14</a></sup> was used for anchoring the genome assembly. A total of 768 sequence-known markers in the genetic map were used to align the scaffolds by BLASTN analysis at an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1 Ã 10<sup>â20</sup>. Hits with coverage &gt;50% and identity &gt;90% were considered mapped markers. The pseudomolecules were constructed on the basis of the anchoring results, and the order of chromosomal numbers was based on the linkage group length in cM.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Identification of repetitive elements in the citrus genome.</h3><p>Transposable element libraries were constructed by integrating the results of different signature-based, homology-based and <i>ab initio</i>âbased methods (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). Results from different methods were combined to build transposable element families as described by Wicker <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2224">46</a></sup>. At least one member for each family was selected to construct the custom transposable element libraries, which were applied to mask the citrus genome using the RepeatMasker software (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction and annotation.</h3><p>A hybrid approach was used, which combined <i>de novo</i> predictions with evidence-based data (ESTs, protein homology and RNA-Seq) analysis using the PASA and EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2247">47</a></sup> pipeline (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). The predicted gene models from EVM were then updated by PASA assembly alignments. Gene functions were assigned according to the best alignment using BLASTP (<i>E</i> value &lt; 10<sup>â5</sup>) to the Uniprot database (including the SWISS-PROT and TrEMBL databases). miRNAs were predicted on the basis of small RNA sequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu, Q. et al. Discovery and comparative profiling of microRNAs in a sweet orange red-flesh mutant and its wild type. BMC Genomics 11, 246 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2260">23</a></sup>, and the criteria applied to characterize a miRNA locus is described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">RNA-Seq analysis.</h3><p>RNA-Seq reads were generated using Illumina and SOLiD platforms and aligned to the reference genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2275">48</a></sup> (v1.2.0) and BioScope (v1.3, with default parameters), respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), to identify exons and splice junctions <i>ab initio</i>. The expression level of each gene in each RNA-Seq library was calculated as the RPKM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B.A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621â628 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2288">42</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). Biological and technical replicates were performed for gene expression analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). For hierarchical clustering analysis, we used the clustering software Cluster 3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="de Hoon, M.J., Imoto, S., Nolan, J. &amp; Miyano, S. Open source clustering software. Bioinformatics 20, 1453â1454 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2298">49</a>) to perform complete linkage hierarchical clustering on genes using uncentered Pearson's correlation as distance measure.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">RNA-PET analysis.</h3><p>RNA-PET analysis for full-length mRNA transcripts was performed as described in Ruan and Ruan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ruan, X. &amp; Ruan, Y. Genome wide full-length transcript analysis using 5â² and 3â² paired-end-tag next generation sequencing (RNA-PET). Methods Mol. Biol. 809, 535â562 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2310">50</a></sup>. Approximately 100 million RNA-PETs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Table 1</a>) were generated from four tissues (callus, flower, leaf and fruit) and were used for mapping, clustering and annotation of the reference genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of potential species-specific genes.</h3><p>The OrthoMCL method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2328">51</a></sup> was adopted to construct gene families for the genes in 22 plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). Proteins with no homologs in the other 21 plant genomes were used for TBLASTN searches against the Plant Unique Transcripts Assembly database (PUTs, <a href="http://www.plantgdb.org/">http://www.plantgdb.org/</a>) to exclude false-positive genes caused by incomplete genome annotation. The potential species-specific genes were rescreened by BLAST searches against the nonredundant protein database in GenBank.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">SNPs and small indels analysis.</h3><p>Illumina reads from heterozygous diploid orange (the diploid parent of the dihaploid line), three mandarin cultivars (<i>C. reticulata</i>) and three pummelo cultivars (<i>C. grandis</i>) were used for SNP and small indel analysis. All the sequencing reads were mapped using bwa v0.5.9 with default parameters for pair-end reads. Two variant calling tools, SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2356">52</a></sup> and the Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2360">53</a></sup>), were used (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). The sequence data from the dihaploid line were used as a negative control for the SNP analysis as described above to estimate the error rate of SNP detection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analyses for the hybrid origin of sweet orange.</h3><p>The SSR and SNP markers were used for genotyping analysis to test the potential hybrid origin of sweet orange.</p><p>For SSR genotyping, SSR regions were predicted using the whole-genome sequence. A total of 480 pairs of SSR primers (primers are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM5">Supplementary Table 24</a>) were selected on the basis of three primer pairs per scaffold (&gt;10 kb). The genomic locations of these SSR loci are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Figure 20</a>. PCR amplification was conducted in a MJ-PTC-200 thermal PCR cycler (MJ Research, Waltham Mass) using the following program: 3 min of 94 Â°C, 32 cycles of 94 Â°C for 30 s, 55 Â°C for 30 s, 72 Â°C for 45 s and a final extension at 72 Â°C for 10 min. After PCR, polyacrylamide gel analysis and SSR bands were visualized with silver staining as described by Chen <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Chen, C. et al. EST-SSR genetic maps for Citrus sinensis and Poncirus trifoliata. Tree Genet. Genomes 4, 1â10 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2391">54</a></sup>.</p><p>For SNP genotyping, the genome-wide patterns of the sweet orange genetic origin were investigated on the basis of the whole-genome SNP data for mandarins, pummelos and sweet orange. We used a sliding window method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nat. Genet. 42, 1027â1030 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2398">55</a></sup> to detect the ratio (denoted as <i>R</i><sub>M/P</sub>) of SNP numbers from mandarin (<sub>M</sub>) or pummelo (<sub>P</sub>) in a 100 kb window size and 10 kb sliding step. Only windows with SNP number â¥30 were used for the genetic origin analysis. If <i>R</i><sub>M/P</sub> was larger than 4, we marked this region as from pummelo, and if <i>R</i><sub>M/P</sub> was smaller than 0.25, we marked the region as from mandarin. Otherwise the region was regarded as undetermined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Estimation of genome duplication.</h3><p>We used two methods to detect the signature of genome duplications. The multicollinearity alignment algorithm MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2428">56</a></sup>, with the gap size set to 15 genes and requiring at least 5 syntenic genes, was used to define a duplicated block. For each duplicate gene pair, the synonymous site divergence value (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) was calculated using Bioperl modules under the Jukes-Cantor corrected model (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genomic synteny analysis.</h3><p>MCscan software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486â488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2447">56</a></sup> was used to identify syntenic regions between <i>C. sinensis</i> and two genomes (<i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>) in Malvidae, two genomes (<i>F. vesca</i> and <i>M. domestica</i>) in Fabidae and <i>V. vinifera</i> with the same parameters as used for the above mentioned genome duplication analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Phylogenetic analysis.</h3><p>Maximum parsimonyâbased phylogeny construction was performed using heuristic searches that consisted of tree bisection and reconnection (TBR) branch swapping in PAUP*4.0b10 soft. Maximum likelihoodâbased method analysis was conducted in RaxML version 7.2.8 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Stamatakis, A., Ludwig, T. &amp; Meier, H. RAxML-III: a fast program for maximum likelihood-based inference of large phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 21, 456â463 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2477">57</a>) using the shared single-copy genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2472#MOESM4">Supplementary Note</a>). DNA sequences of 103 single-copy genes were concatenated, and the GTRGAMMA substitution model was used for the phylogenetic tree construction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Chromosome karyotype analysis.</h3><p>Root tips (3â5 mm) from germinated seeds of Valencia sweet orange and suspended cells from calli of the Valencia sweet orange dihaploid line were pretreated with saturated p-dichlorobenzene solution for 2 h at 20 Â°C, fixed in 3:1 ethanol and acetic acid (v/v) for 24 h and stored at â4 Â°C in 70% ethanol solution. Roots were treated with an enzyme mixture containing 0.25% pectinase (Sigma), 0.25% pectolyase Y-23 (Yakult) and 0.5% cellulose RS (Onozuka) for 60â70 min at 37 Â°C. The meristem of each individual root tip was then squashed on a clean slide in a drop of 60% acetic acid and then in a drop of fixation solution with a fine needle and was then flame dried over an alcohol flame.</p><p>Chromomycin A3 (CMA) and 4â²-6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI) banding pattern analysis was performed according to Guerra <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Guerra, M. Cytogenetics of Rutaceae. V. High chromosomal variability in Citrus species revealed by CMA/DAPI staining. Heredity 71, 234â241 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.2472#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7761e2498">41</a></sup>. The slides were double stained with 0.5 mg/ml of CMA (1 h) and 5 Î¼g/ml of DAPI (30 min) and mounted with in antifade solution of Vectashield (Vector Laboratories, Peterborough, UK). Slides were examined under a Zeiss Scope A1 fluorescence microscope (Zeiss, Germany) equipped with a Hamamatsu digital camera. Gray-scale images were captured for each color channel. Chromosomes in at least ten metaphase cells were measured using Image-Pro Plus 5.0 software (Media Cybernetics).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Accession codes.</h3><p>Genome assembly data has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AJPS00000000">AJPS00000000</a> and the BioProject ID <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA86123">PRJNA86123</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AJPS01000000">AJPS01000000</a>. DNA sequence data, assembly, annotation and the browser of the sweet orange genome are available through our website at <a href="http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange">http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange</a>.</p></div></div></section>

Q.X. coordinated the overall project. X.R. directed sequencing data generation. L.-L.C. led the genome annotation analysis. N.N., D.B. and S.G. assembled the draft genome. D.C., W.-B.J., B.-H.H., J.-W.C., Z.T., F.X., O.J.L., Y.L., X.-W.X. and H.-Y.Z. performed gene annotation, SNP analysis, transcriptome analysis and database management. A.Z., Q.X., H.K., J.C., L.W., Q.H., M.K.B., W.Z., J.-H.L., F.G., H.C., Y.-J.C., J.X., X.Y. and W.-W.G. performed repetitive elements, genome anchor, gene synteny and evolutionary analyses. C.C., H.L., X.G., Y.M. and S.X. performed cytological analyses. M.P.L. and M.L.R. developed the sweet orange linkage map. Q.X. and Y.R. wrote the manuscript with contributions from X.R., L.-L.C., D.C., A.Z., N.N., D.B., C.C., W.-B.J., F.X., H.K., M.L.R. and X.-X.D.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2472/email/correspondent/c1/new">Xiu-Xin Deng</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2472/email/correspondent/c2/new">Yijun Ruan</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
